<script>
	function goToTopic(topic) {
		var topicMap = applySpecialCharacterMapping(topic); 
		var url = "/xpl/topicbrowse.jsp?topic=" + escape(topicMap);
		window.top.location.href = url;
	}
</script>

			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract --><div ng-if="::vm.details.abstract" class="ng-scope">
				<strong>Abstract:</strong>
				<div class="abstract-text ng-binding" ng-bind-html="::vm.details.abstract">This paper demonstrates a new polar transmitter architecture, which uses the digitized envelope signal to control the drain voltage of a switching mode power amplifier (PA). It is based on a novel polar modulation using the constant envelope modulated signal. Among the various constant envelope modulators, the DeltaSigma modulator is chosen for its noise-shaping characteristic. It enables the use of a highly efficient switching amplifier with high linearity. For demonstration, the overall transmitter is implemented and tested with a code-division multiple-access IS-95A signal. The class-D and class-F amplifiers are designed and compared for the optimum operation. The experimental results show that the amplifier with small device size is suitable for this application because of the fast switching requirement. For the class-F amplifier, the measured power-added efficiency is 51.7% at 22.1 dBm and the overall efficiency (considering the amplified quantization noise) is 31%. The adjacent channel power ratios at 885 kHz and 1.98 MHz are lower than -44.9 and -55.6 dBc at the output power range from 10.8 to 22.1 dBm without any pre-distortion techniques. The overall efficiency is improved to 48.6% with a three-level quantized DeltaSigma modulator. The results clearly show that the highly efficient switching mode PA can be controlled efficiently using a digital signal from the DeltaSigma envelope modulation technique.</div>
			</div><!-- end ngIf: ::vm.details.abstract -->			
			<!-- ngIf: ::vm.details.scope -->

<div id="BodyWrapper" class="ArticlePage" xmlns:ieee="http://www.ieeexplore.ieee.org"><div id="article">
<div class="section" id="sec1"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION I.</div><h2>Introduction</h2></div><p>Flexible transmitters handling multimode and multiband are inevitable for the evolution of the wireless communication system. The flexibility of the digital signal processing (DSP)-based system has enabled the multimode operation of the baseband modem chips, but the multimode and multiband operation in the analog and RF area is not familiar yet. Recently, as the CMOS-based DSP technology evolves, there are many efforts to migrate the radio function into the DSP and to control the radio function using the DSP <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref1" id="context_ref_1_1">[1]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref2" id="context_ref_2_1">[2]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref3" id="context_ref_3_1">[3]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref4" id="context_ref_4_1">[4]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref5" id="context_ref_5_1">[5]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref6" id="context_ref_6_1">[6]</a>.</p><p>Digital radio provides many benefits including robustness to variations from component inaccuracies and aging. Moreover, a smaller circuit area from a higher integration level of Si CMOS technology and lower fabrication cost are allowed. As the technology advances toward higher speed of operation, the transmitter, employing a direct-digital synthesizer (DDS), as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig1" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 1</a>, will arrive at the terminal. It directly synthesizes the microwave signal from the digital processor and the only analog component in the transmitter chain is the power amplifier (PA).</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig1"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-1-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-1-small.gif" alt="Figure 1" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 1. </b><fig><p>Next-generation transmitter architecture.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>The PA should be highly linear for the increased bandwidth and peak-to-average power ratio (PAPR) of the signals as the wireless services move to fourth generation (4G) for higher data rate. It also requires high efficiency for long battery lifetime, but the linear operation necessitates the tradeoff with the efficiency, i.e., the approach for the next-generation transmitter should be accompanied by the highly efficient and linear PA. The switching mode PA is so efficient that it is suitable for the portable handset applications requiring long battery time. However, the nonlinear characteristic of the switching amplifier makes it hard to deal with the time-varying envelope signals, and the direct adoption of switching amplifier is inappropriate. The linear amplification using nonlinear components (LINCs) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_1">[10]</a>, bandpass <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-2-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-5" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-6"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-7" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Δ</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-8" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Σ</span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-2">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulation <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">({\hbox{BP}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-3-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-9" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-10"><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-11" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">(</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-12"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-13"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-14"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-15"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-16" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">BP</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-17" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Δ</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-18" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Σ</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-19"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-20"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-21"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-22"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-23" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">M</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mo" id="MathJax-Span-24" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">)</span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-3">({\hbox{BP}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_1">[7]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_1">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_1">[9]</a>, and polar modulator/envelope elimination and restoration (EER) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_1">[12]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_1">[13]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_1">[14]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_1">[15]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_1">[16]</a> enables the linear amplification with the switching amplifier by modulating the input signals or power supply.</p><p>The LINC system generates two constant envelope signals by adding the error signals, then amplifies and combines two signals. Since the error signal is dissipated as heat in the combining process, the efficiency of the system is not high.</p><p>The RF transmitter employing a <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{BP}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-4-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-25" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-26"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-27"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-28"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-29"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-30"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-31" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">BP</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-32" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Δ</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-33" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Σ</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-34"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-35"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-36"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-37"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-38" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">M</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-4">{\hbox{BP}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, seemingly the closest solution for the digital radio, converts a modulated signal into a pulse train in the time domain by adding noise in out-of-band, while preserving the signal. The pulse train can be amplified using a highly efficient switching mode PA. However, the added noise degrades the transmitter efficiency. The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\hbox{BP}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-5-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-39" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-40"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-41"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-42"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-43"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-44"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-45" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">BP</span></span></span></span></span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-46" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Δ</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-47" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">Σ</span><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-48"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-49"><span class="mstyle" id="MathJax-Span-50"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-51"><span class="mtext" id="MathJax-Span-52" style="font-family: MathJax_Main;">M</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-5">{\hbox{BP}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> requires unrealistically high sampling frequency, usually four times the RF carrier frequency. Such a high-speed digital circuit consumes significant power so that the efficiency of the system gets worse.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig2"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-2-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-2-small.gif" alt="Figure 2" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 2. </b><fig><p>Operation principle of constant envelope supply-modulated PA.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p><p>The polar modulator/EER system ideally delivers 100% efficiency even for the high PAPR signals. The switching PA is modulated by the envelope signal through power supply so the high efficiency is maintained with assumption of a 100% amplitude modulator <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_1">[17]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_1">[18]</a>. However, the nonlinear characteristic of the switching amplifier induces some distortion at the output, mainly due to the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{\rm DS}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-6-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-53" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-54"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-55"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.565em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.78em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-56" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">V<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.183em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.841em; left: 0.604em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-57"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-58"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-59" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">D</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-60" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">S</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-6">V_{\rm DS}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>/PM. It is so serious a problem in designing the polar modulator/EER system that the pre-distortion technique is required to minimize the distortion effects. Recently, there are some good results to solve the problem <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref11" id="context_ref_11_1">[11]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref12" id="context_ref_12_1">[12]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref13" id="context_ref_13_1">[13]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref14" id="context_ref_14_1">[14]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref15" id="context_ref_15_1">[15]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref16" id="context_ref_16_1">[16]</a>, but they require complex circuitries for implementation.</p><p>The constant envelope modulator solves the problem by converting the time-varying envelope signal into a just 1-bit information assisted by added noise <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_1">[19]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_1">[20]</a>. Instead of continuously modulating the power supply, it turns the amplifier on and off. The discrete switching action linearly combines the phase signal with the constant envelope modulated signal. The concept of the constant envelope supply-modulated PA is described in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig2" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 2</a>. The number of quantization levels can be increased to reduce the quantization noise using the multibit quantizer. In this case, the nonlinearity from the multilevel <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">V_{\rm DS}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-7-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-61" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-62"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-63"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 1.565em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.186em 1000.78em 4.207em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-64" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">V<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.183em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.841em; left: 0.604em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-65"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-66"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-67" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">D</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-68" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">S</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-7">V_{\rm DS}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> can be easily corrected for its discrete characteristic. In this architecture, any modulated signal is converted to a pulse train and the signal can control a highly efficient switch amplifier in the RF domain. Therefore, the transmitter is easily reconfigured in the digital domain for SDR application.</p><p>In this paper, we have designed and demonstrated the transmitter employing the constant envelope modulator. Although the basic idea has been discussed in <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref21" id="context_ref_21_1">[21]</a>, this study presents a more detailed description and further experimental results. After reviewing various constant envelope modulators and their characteristics with a CDMA IS-95A signal in <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec2" class="fulltext-link">Section II</a>, <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec3" class="fulltext-link">Section III</a> discusses the design of the overall transmitter from the digital baseband processors to the switching mode RF PAs. It presents the optimum design of the PA with switching action. <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec4" class="fulltext-link">Section IV</a> shows the experimental results of the designed transmitter for the CDMA IS-95A signal. In <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec5" class="fulltext-link">Section V</a>, we extend our research to the multibit operation for better performances. No digital pre-distortion technique is applied for this system to purely demonstrate the effect of the constant envelope modulation.</p></div>
<div class="section" id="sec2"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION II.</div><h2>Various Constant Envelope Modulators</h2></div><p>In the proposed transmitter architecture, the PA roles as the signal multiplier in the time domain. The multiplication in the time domain is mathematically the convolution in the frequency domain. Therefore, the constant envelope modulators, which can provide the previously mentioned advantages, produce the convolution output spectrum at the amplifier output. It means the quantization noise in the modulator's output influences to the output spectrum of the PA. It should be carefully decided which one is the optimum for the transmitter applications among the various constant envelope modulators.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec2a"><h3>A. Design Considerations of Constant Envelope Modulators</h3><p>The square-shape pulse of the modulator's output signal contains the quantization noise and the PA amplifies not only the signal, but also the quantization noise. For exact estimation of the efficiency, the portion of the quantization noise should be subtracted from the PA's efficiency. The efficiency of the 1-bit modulator <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eta_{\rm overall}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processed" id="MathJax-Element-8-Frame" tabindex="0"><nobr><span class="math" id="MathJax-Span-69" role="math"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 0.003em; height: 0px; font-size: 111%;"><span style="position: absolute;"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-70"><span class="msubsup" id="MathJax-Span-71"><span style="display: inline-block; position: relative; width: 2.646em; height: 0px;"><span style="position: absolute; clip: rect(3.426em 1000.48em 4.447em -999.997em); top: -4.021em; left: 0.003em;"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-72" style="font-family: MathJax_Math-italic;">η<span style="display: inline-block; overflow: hidden; height: 1px; width: 0.003em;"></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span><span style="position: absolute; top: -3.901em; left: 0.483em;"><span class="texatom" id="MathJax-Span-73"><span class="mrow" id="MathJax-Span-74"><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-75" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">o</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-76" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">v</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-77" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">e</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-78" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">r</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-79" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">a</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-80" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">l</span><span class="mi" id="MathJax-Span-81" style="font-size: 70.7%; font-family: MathJax_Main;">l</span></span></span><span style="display: inline-block; width: 0px; height: 4.027em;"></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></nobr></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-8">\eta_{\rm overall}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is defined as <disp-formula id="deqn1-2" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{\eta_{\rm overall}[\%]=&amp;\,\eta_{\rm mod}\times\eta_{\rm PA}&amp;\hbox{(1)}\cr\eta_{\rm mod}[\%]\cong&amp;\,{{\rm PQPR}\over {\rm PAPR}}\times 100&amp;\hbox{(2)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-9-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-9">\eqalignno{\eta_{\rm overall}[\%]=&amp;\,\eta_{\rm mod}\times\eta_{\rm PA}&amp;\hbox{(1)}\cr\eta_{\rm mod}[\%]\cong&amp;\,{{\rm PQPR}\over {\rm PAPR}}\times 100&amp;\hbox{(2)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{\eta_{\rm overall}[\%]=&amp;\,\eta_{\rm mod}\times\eta_{\rm PA}&amp;\hbox{(1)}\cr\eta_{\rm mod}[\%]\cong&amp;\,{{\rm PQPR}\over {\rm PAPR}}\times 100&amp;\hbox{(2)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eta_{\rm PA}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-10-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-10">\eta_{\rm PA}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the efficiency of the PA. <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eta_{\rm mod}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-11-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-11">\eta_{\rm mod}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> represents the input signal portion contained in the output pulse of the constant envelope modulator. The peak-to-quantization power ratio (PQPR) and PAPR are defined as <disp-formula id="deqn3-4" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{{\rm PQPR}=&amp;\,{{\hbox{permissible peak input signal power}}\over {\hbox{output quantization level power}}}&amp;\hbox{(3)}\cr {\rm PAPR}=&amp;\,{{\hbox{peak input signal power}}\over {\hbox{average input signal power.}}}&amp;\hbox{(4)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-12-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-12">\eqalignno{{\rm PQPR}=&amp;\,{{\hbox{permissible peak input signal power}}\over {\hbox{output quantization level power}}}&amp;\hbox{(3)}\cr {\rm PAPR}=&amp;\,{{\hbox{peak input signal power}}\over {\hbox{average input signal power.}}}&amp;\hbox{(4)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{{\rm PQPR}=&amp;\,{{\hbox{permissible peak input signal power}}\over {\hbox{output quantization level power}}}&amp;\hbox{(3)}\cr {\rm PAPR}=&amp;\,{{\hbox{peak input signal power}}\over {\hbox{average input signal power.}}}&amp;\hbox{(4)}}</span></span></disp-formula>The permissible signal peak power is restricted by the stability of the modulator. Usually, for the modulator employing the low-order loop filter, the permissible signal peak power is the same to the quantization level power. As the order of the loop filter increases, however, the permissible signal peak power decreases. The quantization level power is determined by the quantization level, as the word says. For the envelope signal, which is always positive, the quantization level can be set to 1 and 0 for the two-level quantization. The criteria level can be optimized to minimize the amount of the quantization noise. The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\pm</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-13-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-13">\pm</script></tex-math></inline-formula>1 quantization can be used for ease of combining the envelope and phase signals, which enables just flipping the up-converted phase signal. However, the signal efficiency is lower than the 1/0 quantization since the negative quantization value generates more quantization noise than the 1/0 quantization. The quantization noise can be reduced by the multibit quantization, resulting in the efficiency enhancement over the predicted value from <a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn1-2" href="#deqn1-2" class="fulltext-link">(1)</a>–<a ref-type="disp-formula" anchor="deqn3-4" href="#deqn3-4" class="fulltext-link">(4)</a>. In the case, the PQPR term increases as the output quantization level power decreases.</p><p>The constant envelope modulator is inherently nonlinear due to the existence of the quantizer. It adds the quantization noise to the input signal to make the square shape. The quantization noise influencing the efficiency of the modulator is also related to the spectral linearity. As the quantization noise reduces, the spectral linearity of the output signal gets better. With multibit quantization, the higher oversampling of the signal allows the reduced quantization noise. A more specific mechanism determining the linearity depends on the transfer function of the modulators.</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig3"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-3-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-3-small.gif" alt="Figure 3" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 3. </b><fig><p>Spectra of the envelope PWM and RPWM for CDMA IS-95A signal.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div><p></p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b"><h3>B. Various Constant Envelope Modulators</h3><p>There are many types of modulators, which convert the time-varying envelope signal into the constant envelope signal. Here, pulsewidth modulation (PWM), random pulsewidth modulation (RPWM), <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-14-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-14">\Delta</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulation, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-15-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-15">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulation are reviewed in the aspect of the modulation efficiency and linearity.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec2b1"><h4>1. Pulsewidth Modulator</h4><p>PWM uses a square wave whose duty cycle is modulated according to the variation of the waveform's average value. The absence of a feedback loop enables a high-speed operation of the modulator. Moreover, the amount of quantization noise is not large so that the signal efficiency of the modulator is high. For the CDMA IS-95A signal, the signal efficiency is 75%. However, the PWM waveforms are composed of a component at the desired fundamental frequency and a number of undesired harmonics centered at each integer multiple of the switching frequency, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a>. Thus, the strict post filtering is required to alleviate the harmonics.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b2"><h4>2. Random Pulsewidth Modulator</h4><p>RPWM has evolved to solve the harmonic problem of the PWM. Instead of a sawtooth or a triangle waveform based on a fixed switching frequency, it employs the random number generator and voltage-controlled oscillator. The control voltage of the oscillator varies randomly so that there is no specific switching frequency. It has an effect to remove the discrete harmonics, but the continuous noise level increases. Thus, it has higher quantization noise than the PWM so the signal efficiency gets lower to 62%. The spectra of RPWM compared with PWM is presented in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig3" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 3</a>. The noise level is too high to be useful for the transmitter.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec2b3"><h4>3. <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-16-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-16">\Delta</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-17-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-17">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> Modulator</h4><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-18-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-18">\Delta</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator is a kind of predictive encoder, whose output is based on the difference between a sample of the input and a predicted value of that sample. The advantage of this structure is that larger input signals are allowed because the difference is smaller than the input signal itself for the oversampled signal. However, the order of the loop filter in the feedback path is limited for the stability issue so that the high-order circuitry for an accurate prediction is difficult to design. The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-19-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-19">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a> employs oversampling and noise-shaping techniques to lower the quantization noise <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref22" id="context_ref_22_2b3">[22]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_2b3">[23]</a>. The oversampling occurs whenever a signal is sampled at a frequency larger than twice its bandwidth. It widens the spectrum unnecessarily. Regardless of increased sample rate, however, the quantization noise power remains unchanged. Thus, the oversampling makes the noise power density go down as the spectrum widens. The benefit obtained from the oversampling is supplemented by a filtering operation that shifts part of the noise to a high frequency, leaving less in the baseband. The simple equivalent model described in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig4" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 4</a> shows that the signal and quantization noise have different transfer functions; low-pass filter for the signal and high-pass filter for the quantization noise as <disp-formula id="deqn5-6" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{y=&amp;\,H\cdot(x-y)+e_{\rm rms}&amp;\hbox{(5)}\cr y=&amp;\,H_{x}\cdot x+H_{n}\cdot e_{\rm rms}&amp;\hbox{(6)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-20-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-20">\eqalignno{y=&amp;\,H\cdot(x-y)+e_{\rm rms}&amp;\hbox{(5)}\cr y=&amp;\,H_{x}\cdot x+H_{n}\cdot e_{\rm rms}&amp;\hbox{(6)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{y=&amp;\,H\cdot(x-y)+e_{\rm rms}&amp;\hbox{(5)}\cr y=&amp;\,H_{x}\cdot x+H_{n}\cdot e_{\rm rms}&amp;\hbox{(6)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <disp-formula id="deqn7-8" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{H_{x}=&amp;\,{H\over 1+H}&amp;\hbox{(7)}\cr H_{n}=&amp;\,{1\over 1+H}.&amp;\hbox{(8)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-21-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-21">\eqalignno{H_{x}=&amp;\,{H\over 1+H}&amp;\hbox{(7)}\cr H_{n}=&amp;\,{1\over 1+H}.&amp;\hbox{(8)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{H_{x}=&amp;\,{H\over 1+H}&amp;\hbox{(7)}\cr H_{n}=&amp;\,{1\over 1+H}.&amp;\hbox{(8)}}</span></span></disp-formula>Thus, the oversampling effect can be expressed as follows: <disp-formula id="deqn9" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">e_{0}^{2}=\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}S\left(e_{\rm rms}^{2}\right)df=e_{\rm rms}^{2}{2f_{0}\over f_{s}}={e_{\rm rms}^{2}\over {\rm OSR}}\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-22-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-22">e_{0}^{2}=\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}S\left(e_{\rm rms}^{2}\right)df=e_{\rm rms}^{2}{2f_{0}\over f_{s}}={e_{\rm rms}^{2}\over {\rm OSR}}\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">e_{0}^{2}=\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}S\left(e_{\rm rms}^{2}\right)df=e_{\rm rms}^{2}{2f_{0}\over f_{s}}={e_{\rm rms}^{2}\over {\rm OSR}}\eqno{\hbox{(9)}}</span></span></disp-formula>and the noise within the signal boundary is given by <disp-formula id="deqn10" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{e^{2}=&amp;\,\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}S\left(e_{\rm rms}^{2}\right)\cdot\left\vert H_{n}(jf)\right\vert^{2}df\cr=&amp;\,e_{\rm rms}^{2}{2\over f_{s}}\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}\left\vert H_{n}(jf)\right\vert^{2}df\cr\approx&amp;\,e_{\rm rms}^{2}{\pi^{2n}\over(2n+1)({\rm OSR})^{2n+1}}&amp;\hbox{(10)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-23-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-23">\eqalignno{e^{2}=&amp;\,\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}S\left(e_{\rm rms}^{2}\right)\cdot\left\vert H_{n}(jf)\right\vert^{2}df\cr=&amp;\,e_{\rm rms}^{2}{2\over f_{s}}\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}\left\vert H_{n}(jf)\right\vert^{2}df\cr\approx&amp;\,e_{\rm rms}^{2}{\pi^{2n}\over(2n+1)({\rm OSR})^{2n+1}}&amp;\hbox{(10)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{e^{2}=&amp;\,\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}S\left(e_{\rm rms}^{2}\right)\cdot\left\vert H_{n}(jf)\right\vert^{2}df\cr=&amp;\,e_{\rm rms}^{2}{2\over f_{s}}\int\limits_{0}^{f_{0}}\left\vert H_{n}(jf)\right\vert^{2}df\cr\approx&amp;\,e_{\rm rms}^{2}{\pi^{2n}\over(2n+1)({\rm OSR})^{2n+1}}&amp;\hbox{(10)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">n</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-24-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-24">n</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the order of the loop filter. The above equations clearly show that the high over-sampling ratio (OSR) and loop filter order reduces the quantization noise at the signal boundary so that high signal-to-noise ratio (SNR) is acquired. Note that the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">n</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-25-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-25">n</script></tex-math></inline-formula> does not increase <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eta_{\rm mod}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-26-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-26">\eta_{\rm mod}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, but the SNR, as observed from the simulation results with the CDMA IS-95A signal in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig5" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 5</a>. For <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{ n}=2</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-27-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-27">{ n}=2</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, as the OSR increases from 8 to 100, the amount of quantization noise near the signal boundary effectively decreases. With <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm OSR}=100</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-28-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-28">{\rm OSR}=100</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-29-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-29">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator operates at a 250-MHz sample rate, and it satisfies the regulation for the spectrum emission mask over the transmit band of CDMA IS-95A.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig4"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-4-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-4-small.gif" alt="Figure 4" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 4. </b><fig><p>Operation principle of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-30-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-30">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-modulator.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig5"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-5-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-5-small.gif" alt="Figure 5" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 5. </b><fig><p>Simulated output spectra of 1-bit (two-level) second-order <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-31-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-31">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized transmitter versus OSRs for CDMA IS-95A signal. The noise (closely related to the spectral linearity) reduces as the OSR increases. The ACPR of the signal source is approximately <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-32-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-32">-</script></tex-math></inline-formula>61 dBc at 885-kHz offset.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The multibit quantization in the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-33-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-33">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulation also improves the SNR. For the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">N</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-34-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-34">N</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-bit quantizer, the SNR is defined as follows:<disp-formula id="deqn11" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm SNR}={{\displaystyle{2^{N}\Delta\over 2\sqrt{2}}}\over{\displaystyle{\Delta\over\sqrt{12}}}}=2^{N}\sqrt{1.5}\eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-35-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-35">{\rm SNR}={{\displaystyle{2^{N}\Delta\over 2\sqrt{2}}}\over{\displaystyle{\Delta\over\sqrt{12}}}}=2^{N}\sqrt{1.5}\eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">{\rm SNR}={{\displaystyle{2^{N}\Delta\over 2\sqrt{2}}}\over{\displaystyle{\Delta\over\sqrt{12}}}}=2^{N}\sqrt{1.5}\eqno{\hbox{(11)}}</span></span></disp-formula>In the decibel scale, <disp-formula id="deqn12-13" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{{\hskip-10pt}20\!\cdot\log_{10}({\rm SNR})\!=&amp;\,20\!\left[N\!\cdot\log_{10}(2)\!+\!0.5\!\cdot\log_{10}(1.5)\right]&amp;\hbox{(12)}\cr{\hskip-10pt}({\rm SNR})_{\rm {dB}}\!=&amp;\,6N\!+\!1.8.&amp;\hbox{(13)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-36-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-36">\eqalignno{{\hskip-10pt}20\!\cdot\log_{10}({\rm SNR})\!=&amp;\,20\!\left[N\!\cdot\log_{10}(2)\!+\!0.5\!\cdot\log_{10}(1.5)\right]&amp;\hbox{(12)}\cr{\hskip-10pt}({\rm SNR})_{\rm {dB}}\!=&amp;\,6N\!+\!1.8.&amp;\hbox{(13)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{{\hskip-10pt}20\!\cdot\log_{10}({\rm SNR})\!=&amp;\,20\!\left[N\!\cdot\log_{10}(2)\!+\!0.5\!\cdot\log_{10}(1.5)\right]&amp;\hbox{(12)}\cr{\hskip-10pt}({\rm SNR})_{\rm {dB}}\!=&amp;\,6N\!+\!1.8.&amp;\hbox{(13)}}</span></span></disp-formula>This expression links the number of bits to the SNR. The simulation result in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6(a)</a> demonstrates that the above description is pretty accurate. For 1-bit increment (from two- to four level), there is approximately 6-dB improvement in the noise power spectrum density. In <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig6" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 6(b)</a>, we have shown the noise density at the receive band of the CDMA IS-95A system. One zero at the receive band is included in the loop filter of the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-37-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-37">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator to reduce the noise. In this case, the noise is suppressed approximately 7.5 dB/bit. Combining with the filtering effect of the duplexer positioned behind the PA, the 250-MHz fourth-order <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-38-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-38">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator employing the three-level quantizer satisfies the noise-level requirement at the receive band, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig7" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 7</a>.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig6"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-6-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-6-small.gif" alt="Figure 6" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 6. </b><fig><p>(a) Simulated transmitter (Tx) band output spectra with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm OSR}=32</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-39-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-39">{\rm OSR}=32</script></tex-math></inline-formula> versus quantization levels. (b) Simulated receiver (Rx) band output spectra of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-40-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-40">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized transmitter with <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm OSR}=100</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-41-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-41">{\rm OSR}=100</script></tex-math></inline-formula> versus quantization levels for CDMA IS-95A signal; The noise at Rx band is reduced as the quantization level increases. The order of the loop filter is increased to four by inserting the zero.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig7"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-7-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-7-small.gif" alt="Figure 7" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 7. </b><fig><p>(a) Measured <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S21</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-42-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-42">S21</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the duplexer from CTS Wireless, Elkhart, IN. (b)Spectra of 250-MHz fourth-order three-level <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-43-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-43">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized transmitter simulated with the measured duplexer's <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">S</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-44-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-44">S</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-parameters.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig8"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-8-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-8-small.gif" alt="Figure 8" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 8. </b><fig><p>Proposed transmitter architecture.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig9"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-9-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-9-small.gif" alt="Figure 9" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 9. </b><fig><p>Digital part of the proposed transmitter implemented in FPGA.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>Compared to the other constant envelope modulation techniques mentioned above, the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-45-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-45">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator provides the most suitable performance due to the accurate noise-shaping characteristic. The signal efficiency of the 1-bit <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-46-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-46">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator is 60% at an 80-MHz sample rate for the CDMA IS-95A signal.</p></div></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec3"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION III.</div><h2>Design of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-47-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-47">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-Digitized RF Transmitter</h2></div><p>The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-48-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-48">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized transmitter architecture we have studied is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a>. It takes advantages of relatively low OSR and low-speed digital circuitry by the envelope <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-49-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-49">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulation (EDSM) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref19" id="context_ref_19_3">[19]</a>, <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref20" id="context_ref_20_3">[20]</a>. Even though the sample rate of the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-50-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-50">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized envelope signal is higher than the bandwidth of the complex signal, it is still lower than the RF carrier frequency. Compared to the transmitter architecture employing a bandpass <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-51-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-51">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator (BPDSM) <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref7" id="context_ref_7_3">[7]</a>–<a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref8" id="context_ref_8_3">[8]</a><a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref9" id="context_ref_9_3">[9]</a>, the sampling frequency decreases from a few gigahertz to dozens of megahertz range. Moreover, the role of the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-52-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-52">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator in this case is as a DAC and the main function of the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-53-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-53">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulation is performed in the digital domain, which can provide efficient and precise signal processing. The <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-54-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-54">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized envelope signal turns the switching mode PA on and off and is combined with an RF up-converted phase signal through the PA. The phase modulated input power level and dc bias of the PA can be easily controlled by a field-programmable fate array (FPGA) for linear amplification <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_3">[10]</a>.</p><div class="section_2" id="sec3a"><h3>A. Digital Baseband Processor</h3><p>In the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-55-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-55">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized transmitter architecture, the majority of the functions can be done digitally, except the frequency up-conversion, amplification, and filtering. The high-performance DSP engine is the elementary unit for this architecture. The DSP engine, shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9</a>, creates two types of signals: the envelope and constant envelope I/Q signals. It consists of the interpolation filter, vector translation, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sin/\cos</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-56-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-56">\sin/\cos</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator, and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-57-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-57">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator. The purpose of the interpolation filter is to take advantage of the increased clock frequency, and to suppress all unnecessary replicas of the signal spectrum occurring between the baseband and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">{\rm OSR}\cdot f_{s}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-58-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-58">{\rm OSR}\cdot f_{s}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The cascade of the root-raised cosine (RRC) and comb filter are used as the interpolation filter to up-sample the baseband in-phase/quadrature (I/Q) signal. The vector translation from the I/Q to the polar is conducted by a coordinate rotation digital calculation (CORDIC) processor. It generates the envelope and phase signals, of which the latter is in the range of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">-\pi</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-59-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-59">-\pi</script></tex-math></inline-formula> to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\pi</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-60-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-60">\pi</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The phase signal from the CORDIC is then applied to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sin/\cos</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-61-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-61">\sin/\cos</script></tex-math></inline-formula> block to generate the normalized I/Q signal <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\cos\ \varphi(t)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-62-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-62">\cos\ \varphi(t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\sin\ \varphi(t)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-63-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-63">\sin\ \varphi(t)</script></tex-math></inline-formula> for up-conversion, and the envelope signal is up-sampled again and modulated by the digital <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-64-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-64">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig10"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-10-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-10-small.gif" alt="Figure 10" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 10. </b><fig><p>Single-bit second-order CRFF <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-65-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-65">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-modulator.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig11"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-11-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-11-small.gif" alt="Figure 11" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 11. </b><fig><p>CDMA IS-95A phase signal with various information bits.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>In the noise-shaping loop, all signals are digital, and hence, no internal data conversion is required. For the same reason, the signal processing in the loop is highly accurate, and we do not need to take any analog imperfections into account when predicting the actual behavior of the loop, compared with the conventional switched-capacitor discrete-time <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-66-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-66">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">({\hbox{SC}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-67-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-67">({\hbox{SC}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula> or the continuous time <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-68-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-68">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">({\hbox{CT}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}})</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-69-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-69">({\hbox{CT}}\Delta\Sigma{\hbox{M}})</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. Moreover, for its simple 1-bit characteristic of the output pulse stream, the simple zero-order sample and hold (ZOH) circuit can replace the high-resolution DAC. Even for the multibit modulator, what is needed are the multiple simple ZOH circuits, which are also very simple structures.</p><p>From the simulation, we found that 80-MHz <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">({\rm OSR}=32)</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-70-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-70">({\rm OSR}=32)</script></tex-math></inline-formula> single-bit second-order cascade-of-resonator with a distributed feed-forward (CRFF) low-pass <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-71-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-71">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula> modulator satisfies the minimum linearity requirements for the CDMA IS-95A signal. The block diagram of the modulator is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig10" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 10</a>, whose coefficients are generated using Matlab <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref23" id="context_ref_23_3a">[23]</a>.</p></div><div class="section_2" id="sec3b"><h3>B. Analog and RF Processors</h3><p>The analog/RF parts of the transmitter consist of two DACs, pulse amplifier, quadrature modulator, variable gain amplifier, and switching mode PA, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig8" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 8</a>. For the phase path, two DACs operate with a 20-MHz update rate. From the simulation, we have found that more than 10 bits are needed to represent the phase information for CDMA IS-95A. The spectra with various information bits are presented in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig11" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 11</a>. The quadrature modulator upconverts the normalized I/Q signals and drives the variable gain amplifier. The input power levels and dc bias of the PA can be controlled by the FPGA through dc-to-dc converters <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref10" id="context_ref_10_3b">[10]</a>. For the envelope path, the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-72-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-72">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized envelope pulse stream is amplified to the level suitable for turning on and off the PA. An inverter can act as the pulse amplifier with an appropriate driving current for a high slew rate switching action, as shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig12" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 12</a>. From the relation between the voltage and current with the capacitive charging, the slew rate is <disp-formula id="deqn14" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\eqalignno{{\rm SR}= \,{dV_{\rm in}\over dt}=\,{i\over C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}} &amp;&amp;\hbox{(14)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-73-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-73">\eqalignno{{\rm SR}= \,{dV_{\rm in}\over dt}=\,{i\over C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}} &amp;&amp;\hbox{(14)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">\eqalignno{{\rm SR}= \,{dV_{\rm in}\over dt}=\,{i\over C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}} &amp;&amp;\hbox{(14)}}</span></span></disp-formula>where <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm gs}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-74-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-74">C_{\rm gs}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the gate–source capacitance of the switching device and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm pa}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-75-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-75">C_{\rm pa}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is the drain–source capacitance of the main amplifier device. When <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-76-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-76">C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> is fixed for certain devices, the available switching speed is determined by the driving current. Similarly, when there is a limitation to the driving current, <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-77-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-77">C_{\rm gs}//C_{\rm pa}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> should be small. However, the on resistance of the switch is inversely proportional to the size of the device, which is proportional to <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm gs}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-78-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-78">C_{\rm gs}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>, and there should be a compromise between the switching speed and device size.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig12"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-12-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-12-small.gif" alt="Figure 12" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 12. </b><fig><p>Pulse driving to switch.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p><p>The switching mode PA for the <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">\Delta\Sigma</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-79-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-79">\Delta\Sigma</script></tex-math></inline-formula>-digitized transmitter should be turned on and off at a high sample rate. The key design factor is to deliver the pulse signal to the PA, while isolating the amplified RF signal from the bias line. The ratio of the pulse to the carrier frequency is more than 10%, and the usage of the choke inductor on the bias line prevents the pulse signal from being delivered to the drain node. Moreover, the capacitances on the bias line can cause slewing of the pulse signal. The minimum pulsewidth <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">T</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-80-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-80">T</script></tex-math></inline-formula> should be larger than the sum of rising time <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">t_{R}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-81-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-81">t_{R}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and falling time <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">t_{F}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-82-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-82">t_{F}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> of the pulse <disp-formula id="deqn15" class="display-formula"><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">T&gt;{t_{R}}+{t_{F}}.\eqno{\hbox{(15)}}</span><div class="MathJax_Display MathJax_Processing"><span class="MathJax" id="MathJax-Element-83-Frame" tabindex="0"></span></div><script type="math/tex; mode=display" id="MathJax-Element-83">T&gt;{t_{R}}+{t_{F}}.\eqno{\hbox{(15)}}</script></tex-math><span class="formula"><span class="link">View Source</span><img aria-describedby="qtip-0" style="display:inline;" title="Right-click on figure or equation for MathML and additional features." data-hasqtip="0" class="qtooltip moreInfo" alt="Right-click on figure for MathML and additional features." src="/assets/img/icon.support.gif" border="0" height="20" width="24" /><span class="tex tex2jax_ignore" style="display:none;">T&gt;{t_{R}}+{t_{F}}.\eqno{\hbox{(15)}}</span></span></disp-formula>If the capacitance is large enough to overrule the above condition, there is a power loss and, hence, the efficiency is degraded. The MOS switch with low on resistance is positioned at the drain to turn on and off the PA. The on resistance causes the voltage drop through the switch device, which results in efficiency degradation.</p><p>The class-D PA employs the transformer whose center tap can be used for drain biasing. It basically has a push–pull architecture so the drain bias node forms a virtual ground. It means that the fundamental frequency component does not exist at this point, while the second harmonic is shorted by the small capacitor <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref18" id="context_ref_18_3b">[18]</a>. The pulse signal is now delivered to the drain without any interferences, and turns the PA on and off. The schematic of the class-D PA with a control switch is shown in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig13" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 13</a>. However, the push–pull architecture inherently has three capacitances on the bias line; two device capacitances <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm ds}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-84-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-84">C_{\rm ds}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and second harmonic short capacitance. The high speed switching is difficult to achieve for the limited slew rate by these capacitances, i.e., the increase of <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">t_{R}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-85-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-85">t_{R}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> and <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">t_{F}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-86-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-86">t_{F}</script></tex-math></inline-formula>. The class-F PA is very similar with the class-D PA, class-B bias, and harmonic tuning network, except that it has just one device for amplification <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref17" id="context_ref_17_3b">[17]</a>. In fact, the class-F amplifier is not a switching amplifier, but a saturation amplifier. By overdriving the amplifier, it generates harmonics and controls them using the harmonic tuning network. The harmonic tuning network consists of the harmonic trap circuits and the tuning line for compensating the detuning effect of the device parasitic components <a ref-type="bibr" anchor="ref24" id="context_ref_24_3b">[24]</a>. The drain bias voltage is supplied through the quarter-wavelength line. <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig14" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 14</a> shows the schematic of the class-F PA with the MOS switch. Compared to the class-D PA, it has the reduced device capacitance so that the slewing effect by <inline-formula id=""><tex-math notation="TeX"><span class="MathJax_Preview">C_{\rm ds}</span><span class="MathJax MathJax_Processing" id="MathJax-Element-87-Frame" tabindex="0"></span><script type="math/tex" id="MathJax-Element-87">C_{\rm ds}</script></tex-math></inline-formula> decreases by one-half. In <a ref-type="sec" anchor="sec4" class="fulltext-link">Section IV</a>, the implementation and measurement results are shown and compared for each amplifier.
</p><div class="figure figure-full" id="fig13"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-13-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-13-small.gif" alt="Figure 13" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 13. </b><fig><p>Class-D PA with control switch.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig14"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-14-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-14-small.gif" alt="Figure 14" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 14. </b><fig><p>Class-F PA with control switch.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<div class="figure figure-full" id="fig15"><!--          Workaround for combined images.Eg.- 1000116 Fig. 5        --><div class="img-wrap"><a href="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-15-large.gif"><img src="/mediastore/IEEE/content/media/22/4387657/4381295/4381295-fig-15-small.gif" alt="Figure 15" /><div class="zoom" title="View Larger Image"></div></a></div><div class="figcaption"><b class="title">Fig. 15. </b><fig><p>DC power consumption of the implemented PA and FPGA.</p></fig></div><p class="links"><a href="/document/4381295/all-figures" class="all">View All</a></p></div>
<p></p></div></div>
<div class="section" id="sec4"><div class="header article-hdr"><div class="kicker">		                        SECTION IV.</div><h2>Implementation and Measurement Results</h2></div><p>The overall functions in <a ref-type="fig" anchor="fig9" class="fulltext-link">Fig. 9</a> are realized with a high-performance FPGA board employing Xilinx Virtex-4. The implemented FPGA, manufactured in 1.2-V 90-nm triple-oxide technology, consumes approximately 18 mW at the 80-MHz sample rate. The power consumption is already low, but can be further reduced by optimizing the circuit.
